which many people still regard as a different breed -- a want of knowledge frequently turn to account by the unscrupulous dealer who be able to sell under the name of aberdeen a dog too bad to dispose of as a scottish terrier but there can be no doubt that originally there must have be _ some _ reason for the name in a letter to the writer sir paynton pigott say some people call they and advertise they as the aberdeen terrier which be altogether a mistake but the reason of it be that forty year ago a dr van bust who live in aberdeen breed these terrier to a large extent and sell they and those be suffer from the ill-effect of too much inbreeding he be not one of the greatest sinner the scottish terrier club be form in the year 1882 in the same year a joint committee draw up a standard of perfection for the breed messr j b morison and thomson gray two gentleman who be look upon as great authority have a good deal to do with it \* \* \* \* \* standard of point of the scottish terrier : skull -- proportionately long slightly domed and cover with short hard hair about 3\/4 inch long or less it should not be quite flat as there should be a sort of stop or drop between the eye muzzle -- very powerful and gradually taper towards the nose which should always be black and of a good size the jaw should be perfectly level and the tooth square though the nose project somewhat over the mouth which give the impression of the upper jaw be longer than the under one eye -- a dark-brown or hazel color small pierce very bright and rather sunken ear -- very small prick or half prick -lrb- the former be preferable -rrb- but never drop they should also be sharp pointed and the hair on they should not be long but velvety and they should not be cut the ear should be free from any fringe at the top neck -- short thick and muscular strongly set on sloping shoulder chest -- broad in comparison to the size of the dog and proportionately deep body -- of moderate length but not so long as a skye 's and rather flat-sided well rib up and exceedingly strong in hind-quarter leg and foot -- both fore and hind leg should be short and very heavy in bone the former be straight and well set on under the body as the scottish terrier should not be out at elbow the hock should be bent and the thigh very muscular and the foot strong small and thickly cover with short hair the fore foot be larger than the hind one tail -- should be about 7 inch long never dock carry with a slight bend and often gaily coat -- should be rather short -lrb- about 2 inch -rrb- intensely hard and wiry in texture and very dense all over the body size -- from 15 lb to 20 lb the best weight be as near as possible 18 lb for dog and 16 lb for bitch when in condition for work color -- steel or iron gray black brindle brown brindle gray brindle black sandy and wheaten white marking be objectionable and can only be allow on the chest and to a small extent general appearance -- the face should wear a very sharp bright and active expression and the head should be carry up the dog -lrb- owe to the shortness of he coat -rrb- should appear to be higher on the leg than he really be but at the same time he should look compact and possess of great muscle in he hind-quarter in fact a scottish terrier though essentially a terrier can not be too powerfully put together and should be from about 9 inch to 12 inch in height special fault : muzzle -- either under or over hung eye -- large or light-coloured ear -- large round at the point or drop it be also a fault if they be too heavily cover with hair leg -- bent or slightly bent and out at elbow coat -- any silkiness wave or tendency to curl be a serious blemish as be also a open coat size -- specimen of over 20 lb should be discourage \* \* \* \* master and he master 's household and be withal a capable and reliable guard he be as a rule a game attractive terrier with heap of brain power and from a show point of view there be always some white west highland terrier be keep at poltalloch sixty year ago and so they be first show as poltalloch terrier yet although they be keep in they purest strain in argyllshire they be still to be find all along the west coast of scotland good specimen belong to ross-shire to skye and at ballachulish on loch leven so that it be a breed with a long pedigree and not a invent breed of the present day emphatically they be not simply white color scottish terrier and it be a error to judge they on scottish terrier line they be smaller than the average scottie more foxy in general conformation -- straight limbed rather long rather low and active in body with a broad forehead light muzzle and underjaw and a bright small intelligent eye colonel malcolm of poltalloch who be recognize as the great authority on the breed lay stress upon the quality of the coat the outer coat he say should be very soft on the forehead and get gradually harder towards the haunch but the harsh coat beloved of the show bench be all nonsense and be the easiest thing in the world to ` fake ' as anyone can try who will dip he own hair into the now fashionable ` anturic ' bath the outer coat should be distinctly _ long _ but not long in the ` fancy ' or show sense still it should be long enough to hang as a thatch over the soft woolly real coat of the animal and keep it dry so that a good shake or two will throw off most of the water while the under coat should be so thick and naturally oily that the dog can swim through a fair-sized river and not get wet or be able to sit out through a drenching rain guard something of he master 's and be none the worse this under coat i at least have never see a judge look for but for the work terrier it be most important the size of the dog be perhaps best indicate by weight the dog should not weigh more than 18 lb nor the bitch more than 16 lb there be among judge i find -- with all respect i say it -- a undue regard for weight and what be call strength also for groom which mean brushing or pluck out all the long hair to gratify the judge one might as well judge of sandow 's strength not by he performance but by the kind of wax he put on he moustache the west highland terrier of the old sort -- i do not of course speak of bench dog -- earn they living follow fox badger or otter wherever these go underground between over or under rock that no man could get at to move and some of such size that a hundred man could not move they -lrb- and oh the beauty of they note when they come across the right scent -rrb- i want my reader to understand this and not to think of a highland fox-cairn as if it be a english fox-earth dig in sand nor of badger work as if it be a question of locate the badger and then digging he out no the badger make he home amongst rock the small one perhaps two or three ton in weight and probably he have he ` hinner end ' against one of three or four hundred ton -- no digging he out -- and moreover the passage between the rock must be take as they be no scratch they a little wider so if you dog 's rib be a trifle too big he may crush one or two through the narrow slit and then stick he will never be able to pull himself back -- at least until starvation have so reduce he that he will probably be unable if set free to win -lrb- as we say in scotland -rrb- he way back to the open i remember a tale of one of my father 's terrier who get so lose the keeper go daily to the cairn hope against hope at last one day a pair of bright eye be see at the bottom of a hole they do not disappear when the dog 's name be call a brilliant idea seize one of the keeper the dog evidently could not get up so a rabbit skin be fold into a small parcel round a stone and let down by a string the dog at once seize the situation -- and the skin -- hold on be draw up and faint on reach the mouth of the hole he be carry home tenderly and nurse he recover refer to the characteristic of this terrier colonel malcolm continue : -- attention to breed as to color have undoubtedly increase the whiteness but other point be good a dog of the west highland white terrier breed be not to be reject if he show he descent by a slight degree of pale red or yellow on he back or he ear i know a old argyllshire family who consider that to improve they terrier they ought all to have browny yellow ear neither again except for the show bench be there the slightest objection to half drop ear -- _ ie _ the point of one or both ear just fall over unfortunately the show bench have a great tendency to spoil all breed from too much attention be give to what be evident -- and ear be grand thing for judge to pin they faith to also they greatly admire a fine long face and what be call -- but wrongly call -- a strong jaw meaning by that a ugly heavy face i have often point out that the tiger the cat the otter all animal remarkable for they strength of jaw have exceedingly short face but they bite be cruelly hard and what again could be daintier than the face of a fox the terrier of the west highland of scotland have come down to the present day build on what i may perhaps call the fox line and it be a type evolve by work -- hard and deadly dangerous work it be only of late year that dog have be breed for show the so-called ` scottish ' terrier which at present rule the roost date from 1879 as a show dog i therefore earnestly hope that no fancy will arise about these dog which will make they less hardy less wise less companionable less active or less desperate fighter underground than they be at present a young dog that i give to a keeper get its stomach tear open in a fight it come out of the cairn to its master to be help he put the entrails back to the best of he ability and then the dog slip out of he hand to finish the fight and force the fox out into the open that be the spirit of the breed but alas that can not be exhibit on the show bench they do say that a keeper of mine when chaff by the ` fancy ' about the baby face of he ` lot ' be drive to ask ` well can any of you gentleman oblige I with a cat and i 'll show you ' i do not hear he say it so it may only be a tale anyhow i have in my kennel a dog who at ten month old meet a vixen fox as she be bolt out of she cairn and he at once catch she by the throat stick to she till the pack come up and then on till she be kill in the course of one month he wound be heal and he have two other classical fight one with a cat and the other with a dog fox not bad for a pup with a ` baby face ' i trust my reader understand that the west highland white terrier be not white aberdeen not a new invention but have a most respectable ancestry of they own i add the formal list of point but this be the work of show bench expert -- and it will be see from what i have write that i do not agree with they on certain particulars there should be feather to a fair degree on the tail but if expert will not allow it put rosin on you hand and pull the hair out -- and the rosin will win you prize the eye should not be sink which give the sulky look of the ` scotch ' terrier but should be full and bright and the expression friendly and confide the skull should not be narrow anywhere it be almost impossible to get black nail in a dog of pure breed and the black soon wear off the pad work so folk must understand this on two occasion recently i have show dog acknowledge as dog to be quite first class ` but you see they be not the proper type ' the judge unfortunately have as yet they eye fill with the ` scottish ' terrier type and prefer mongrel that show it to the real ` simon pure ' \* \* \* \* \* standard of point : the general appearance of the west highland white terrier be that of a small game hardy-looking terrier possess with no small amount of self-esteem with a varminty appearance strongly build deep in chest and back rib straight back and powerful quarter on muscular leg and exhibit in a marked degree a great combination of strength and activity color -- white coat -- very important and seldom see to perfection must be double-coated the outer coat consist of hard hair about 2-1\/2 inch long and free from any curl the under coat which resemble fur be short soft and close open coat be objectionable size -- dog to weigh from 14 to 18 lb and bitch from 12 to 16 lb and measure from 8 to 12 inch at the shoulder skull -- should not be too narrow be in proportion to he powerful jaw proportionately long slightly domed and gradually taper to the eye between which there should be a slight indentation or stop eyebrow heavy the hair on the skull to be from 3\/4 to 1 inch long and fairly hard eye -- widely set apart medium in size dark hazel in color slightly sink in the head sharp and intelligent which look from under the heavy eyebrow give a pierce look full eye and also light-coloured eye be very objectionable muzzle -- should be powerful proportionate in length and should gradually taper towards the nose which should be fairly wide and should not project forward beyond the upper jaw the jaw level and powerful and tooth square or evenly meet well set and large for the size of the dog the nose and roof of mouth should be distinctly black in color ear -- small carry erect or semi-erect but never drop and should be carry tightly up the semi-erect ear should drop nicely over at the tip the break be about three-quarter up the ear and both form of ear should terminate in a sharp point the hair on they should be short smooth -lrb- velvety -rrb- and they should not be cut the ear should be free from any fringe at the top round point broad and large ear be very objectionable also ear too heavily cover with hair neck -- muscular and nicely set on sloping shoulder chest -- very deep with breadth in proportion to the size of the dog body -- compact straight back rib deep and well arched in the upper half of rib present a flattish side appearance loin broad and strong hind-quarter strong muscular and wide across the top leg and foot -- both fore and hind leg should be short and muscular the shoulder blade should be comparatively broad and well-sloped backwards the point of the shoulder blade should be closely knit into the backbone so that very little movement of they should be noticeable when the dog be walk the elbow should be close in to the body both when move or stand thus cause the fore-leg to be well place in under the shoulder the fore-leg should be straight and thickly cover with short hard hair the hind-leg should be short and sinewy the thigh very muscular and not too wide apart the hock bent and well set in under the body so as to be fairly close to each other either when stand walk or run -lrb- trot -rrb- and when stand the hind-leg from the point of the hock down to fetlock joint should be straight or perpendicular and not far apart the fore-foot be larger than the hind one be round proportionate in size strong thickly pad and cover with short hard hair the foot must point straight forward the hind-foot be smaller not quite as round as fore-foot and thickly pad the under surface of the pad of foot and all the nail should be distinctly black in color hock too much bent -lrb- cow hock -rrb- detract from the general appearance straight hock be weak both kind be undesirable and should be guard against tail -- six or seven inch long cover with hard hair no feather as straight as possible carry gaily but not curl over back a long tail be objectionable movement -- should be free straight and easy all round in front the leg should be freely extend forward by the shoulder the hind movement should be free strong and close the hock should be freely flex and draw close in under the body so that when move off the foot the body be throw or push forward with some force stiff stilty movement behind be very objectionable fault : coat -- any silkiness wave or tendency to curl be a serious blemish as be also a open coat black or gray hair disqualify for competition size -- any specimen under the minimum or above the maximum weight be objectionable eye -- full or light color ear -- round-pointed drop broad and large or too heavily cover with hair muzzle -- either under or over shot and defective tooth \* \* \* \* \* -lrb- illustration : col malcolm 's west highland white terrier sonny and sarah -rrb- -lrb- illustration : miss e mccheane 's skye terrier ch fairfield diamond and ch wolverley chummie photograph by t fall -rrb- chapter xl the dandie dinmont the breed of terrier now know as the dandie dinmont be one of the race of the dog which can boast of a fairly ancient lineage though it be impossible now to say what be the exact origin of this breed we know that it be first recognize under its present name after the publication of scott 's _ guy manner _ in the year 1814 and we know that for many year previously there have exist in the border county a rough-haired short-legged race of terrier the constant and very effective companion of the border farmer and other in they fox-hunting expedition various theory have be suggest by different writer as to the manner in which the breed be found some say that the dandie be the result of cross a strain of rough-haired terrier with the dachshund other that a rough-haired terrier be cross with the otterhound and other again assert that no direct cross be ever introduce to find the breed but that it be gradually evolve from the rough-haired terrier of the border district and this latter theory be probably correct the dandie would appear to be closely related to the bedlington terrier in both breed we find the same indomitable pluck the same pendulous ear and a light silky topknot adorning the skull of each but the dandie be evolve into a long-bodied short-legged dog and the bedlington become a long-legged short-bodied dog indeed to illustrate the close relationship of the two breed a case be quote of the late lord antrim who in the early day of dog show exhibit two animal from the same litter and with the one obtain a prize or honorable mention in the dandie class and with the other a like distinction in the bedlington class it may be interesting to give a few particulars concern the traceable ancestor of the modern dandie in mr charle cook 's book on this breed we be give particulars of one william allan of holystone bear in 1704 and know as piper allan and celebrate as a hunter of otter and fox and for he strain of rough-haired terrier who so ably assist he in the chase william allan 's terrier descend to he son jame also know as the piper and bear in the year 1734 jame allan die in 1810 and be survive by a son who sell to mr franci somner at yetholm a terrier dog name old pepper descend from he grandfather 's famous dog hitchem old pepper be the great-grandsire of mr somner 's well-known dog shem these terrier belong to the allan and other in the district be consider by mr cook to be the earliest known ancestor of the modern dandie dinmont sir walter scott himself inform we that he do not draw the character of dandie dinmont from any one individual in particular but that the character would well fit a dozen or more of the lidderdale yeoman of he acquaintance however owe to the circumstance of he call all he terrier mustard and pepper without any other distinction except auld and young and little the name come to be fix by he associate upon one jame davidson of hindlee a wild farm in the teviotdale mountain jame davidson die in the year 1820 by which time the dandie dinmont terrier be be breed in considerable number by the border farmer and other to meet the demand for it which have spring up since the appearance of _ guy manner _ as a result of the controversy that be continually recur with regard to the point of a typical dandie dinmont there be form in the year 1876 the dandie dinmont terrier club with the object of settle the question for ever and for this purpose all the most note breeder and other interested be invite to give they view upon it the standard of point adopt by the club be as follow : -- \* \* \* \* \* head -- strongly make and large not out of proportion to the dog 's size the muscle show extraordinary development more especially the maxillary skull -- broad between the ear get gradually less towards the eye and measure about the same from the inner corner of the eye to back of skull as it do from ear to ear the forehead well dome the head be cover with very soft silky hair which should not be confine to a mere topknot and the lighter in color and silkier it be the better the cheek start from the ear proportionately with the skull have a gradual taper towards the muzzle which be deep and strongly make and measure about three inch in length or in proportion to skull as three be to five the muzzle be cover with hair of a little darker shade than the topknot and of the same texture as the feather of the fore-leg the top of the muzzle be generally bare for about a inch from the black part of the nose the bareness come to a point towards the eye and be about one inch broad at the nose the nose and inside of mouth black or dark color the tooth very strong especially the canine which be of extraordinary size for such a small dog the canine fit well into each other so as to give the greatest available holding and punish power and the tooth be level in front the upper one very slightly overlap the under one -lrb- many of the finest specimen have a swine mouth which be very objectionable but it be not so great a objection as the protrusion of the under jaw -rrb- eye -- set wide apart large full round bright expressive of great determination intelligence and dignity set low and prominent in front of the head color a rich dark hazel ear -- pendulous set well back wide apart and low on the skull hang close to the cheek with a very slight projection at the base broad at the junction of the head and taper almost to a point the fore part of the ear taper very little the taper be mostly on the back part the fore part of the ear come almost straight down from its junction with the head to the tip they should harmonise in color with the body color in the case of a pepper dog they be cover with a soft straight brownish hair -lrb- in some case almost black -rrb- in the case of a mustard dog the hair should be mustard in color a shade darker than the body but not black all should have a thin feather of light hair start about two inch from the tip and of nearly the same color and texture as the topknot which give the ear the appearance of a distinct point the animal be often one or two year old before the feather be show the cartilage and skin of the ear should not be thick but rather thin length of ear from three to four inch neck -- very muscular well develop and strong show great power of resistance be well set into the shoulder body -- long strong and flexible rib well spring and round chest well develop and let well down between the fore-leg the back rather low at the shoulder have a slight downward curve and a corresponding arch over the loin with a very slight gradual drop from top of loin to root of tail both side of backbone well supply with muscle tail -- rather short say from eight inch to ten inch and cover on the upper side with wiry hair of darker color than that of the body the hair on the under side be lighter in color and not so wiry with a nice feather about two inch long get shorter as it near the tip rather thick at the root get thicker for about four inch then taper off to a point it should not be twist or curl in any way but should come up with a curve like a scimitar the tip when excite be in a perpendicular line with the root of the tail it should neither be set on too high nor too low when not excite it be carry gaily and a little above the level of the body leg -- the fore-leg short with immense muscular development and bone set wide apart the chest come well down between they the foot well form and _ not flat _ with very strong brown or dark-coloured claw bandy leg and flat foot be objectionable the hair on the fore-leg and foot of a pepper dog should be tan vary accord to the body color from a rich tan to a pale fawn of a mustard dog they be of a darker shade than its head which be a creamy white in both color there be a nice feather about two inch long rather lighter in color than the hair on the fore-part of the leg the hind-leg be a little longer than the fore one and be set rather wide apart but not spread out in a unnatural manner while the foot be much smaller the thigh be well develop and the hair of the same color and texture as the fore one but have no feather or dew claw the whole claw should be dark but the claw of all vary in shade accord to the color of the dog 's body coat -- this be a very important point the hair should be about two inch long that from skull to root of tail a mixture of hardish and soft hair which give a sort of crisp feel to the hand the hair should not be wiry the coat be term pily or pencil the hair on the under part of the body be lighter in color and softer than that on the top the skin on the belly accord with the color of dog color -- the color be pepper or mustard the pepper range from a dark bluish black to a light silver gray the intermediate shades be prefer the body color come well down the shoulder and hip gradually merge into the leg color the mustard vary from a reddish brown to a pale fawn the head be a creamy white the leg and foot of a shade darker than the head the claw be dark as in other color -lrb- nearly all dandie dinmont have some white on the chest and some have also white claw -rrb- size -- the height should be from 8 to 11 inch at the top of shoulder length from top of shoulder to root of tail should not be more than twice the dog 's height but preferably one or two inch less weight -- from 14 lb to 24 lb the best weight as near 18 lb as possible these weight be for dog in good work order \* \* \* \* \* in the above standard of point we have a very full and detailed account of what a dandie should be like and if only judge at type of the breed a dandie make a excellent house guard for such a small dog he have a amazingly deep loud bark so that the stranger who have hear he bark on the far side of the door be quite astonished when he see the small owner of the big voice when keep as a companion he become a most devoted and affectionate little friend and be very intelligent as a dog to be keep in kennel there be certainly one great drawback where large kennel be desire and that be the risk of keep two or more dog in one kennel sooner or later there be sure to be a fight and when dandy fight it be generally a very serious matter if no one be present to separate they one or both of the combatant be pretty certain to be kill but when out walk the dandie be no more quarrelsome than other breed of terrier if properly train from puppyhood there be one little matter in breed dandy that be generally a surprise to the novice and that be the very great difference in the appearance of the young pup and the adult dog the pup be bear quite smooth-haired the pepper be black and tan in color and the mustard have a great deal of black in they color the topknot begin to appear sometimes when the dog be a few month old and sometimes not till he be a year or so old it be generally best to mate a mustard to a pepper to prevent the mustard become too light in color though two rich-coloured mustard may be mate together with good result it be a rather curious fact that when two mustard be mate some of the progeny be usually pepper in color though when two pepper be mate there be very seldom any mustard puppy the popularity of the dandie have now last for nearly a hundred year and there be no reason why it should not last for another century if breeder will only steer clear of the exaggeration of show point and continue to breed a sound active and hardy terrier chapter xli the skye and clydesdale terrier that the skye terrier should be call the heavenly breed be a tribute to the favor in which he popularity far in advance of that attain by any other breed of toy dog one of the first act of the club be to draw up a standard of point as follow : -- \* \* \* \* \* appearance -- the pomeranian should be a compact short couple dog well knit in frame he should exhibit great intelligence in he expression and activity and buoyancy in he deportment head and nose -- should be foxy in outline or wedge-shaped the skull be slightly flat large in proportion to the muzzle which should finish rather fine and free from lippiness the tooth should be level and should on no account be undershoot the hair on the head and face should be smooth and short-coated the nose should be black in white orange and sable dog but in other color may be self but never parti-colour or white ear -- should be small not set too far apart nor too low down but carry perfectly erect like those of a fox and like the head should be cover with short soft hair eye -- should be medium in size not full nor set too wide apart bright and dark in color show great intelligence in white shaded sable or orange dog the rim round the eye should be black neck and body -- the neck should be rather short well set in the back must be short and the body compact be well rib up and the barrel well round the chest must be fairly deep and not too wide but in proportion to the size of the dog leg -- the fore-leg must be well feathered perfectly straight of medium length and not such as would be term leggy or low on leg but in due proportion in length and strength to a well-balanced frame must be fine in bone and free in action the hind-leg and thigh must be well feathered neither contract nor wide behind the foot small and compact in shape shoulder should be clean and well lay back tail -- the tail be one of the characteristic of the breed and should be turn over the back and carry flat and straight be profusely cover with long harsh spreading hair coat -- there should be two coat a undercoat and a overcoat the one a soft fluffy undercoat the other a long perfectly straight coat harsh in texture cover the whole of the body be very abundant round the neck and fore part of the shoulder and chest where it should form a frill of profuse standing off straight hair extend over the shoulder the hind-quarter should be clothe with long hair or feather from the top of the rump to the hock color -- all whole color be admissible but they should be free from white or shading and the whites must be quite free from lemon or any other color a few white hair in any of the self color shall not necessarily disqualify at present the whole color dog be : -- white black brown -lrb- light or dark -rrb- blue -lrb- as pale as possible -rrb- orange -lrb- which should be as deep and even in color as possible -rrb- beaver or cream dog other than white with white foot or foot leg or leg be decidedly objectionable and should be discourage and can not compete as whole color specimen in parti-coloured dog the color should be evenly distribute on the body in patch a dog with white or tan foot or chest would not be a parti-colour shaded sable should be shaded throughout with three or more color the hair to be as uniformly shaded as possible with no patch of self color in mixed class where whole color and parti-coloured pomeranian compete together the preference should if in other point they be equal be give to the whole color specimen where classification be not by color the following be recommend for adoption by show committee : -- 1 not exceed 7 lb -lrb- pomeranian miniature -rrb- 2 exceed 7 lb -lrb- pomeranian -rrb- 3 pomeranian and pomeranian miniature mixed \* \* \* \* \* the early type of a pomeranian be that of a dog vary from 10 lb or 12 lb weight up to 20 lb weight or even more and admirable quality will always make the pomeranian a favorite both in the cottage and in the palace chapter xliv the king charle spaniel in the fourth chapter of macaulay 's _ history of england _ we read of king charle ii that he might be see before the dew be off the grass in st jame 's park striding among the tree play with he spaniel and fling corn to he duck and these exhibition endear he to the common people who always like to see the great unbend queen elizabeth 's physician dr caius describe these little spaniel as delicate neate and pretty kind of dogge call the spaniel gentle or the dally withall to tryfle away the treasure of time to withdraw they minde from they commendable exercise these puppy the smaller they be the more pleasure they provoke as more meete playfellowe for minse mistriss to beare in they bosom to keepe company withall in they chamber to succor with sleepe in bed and nourishe with meate at board to lie in they lappe and licke they lippe as they ryde in they waggon and good reason it should be so for coursenesse with fynenesse have no fellowship but featnesse with neatnesse have neighborhood enough there would appear to be much divergence of opinion as to the origin of this breed and the date of its first appearance in england but it be certainly acclimatise here as early as the reign of henry viii and it be generally think that it be of japanese origin take from japan to spain by the early voyager to the east and thence import into england the english toy spaniel of to-day especially the blenheim variety be also say by some to be relate to some sport spaniel which belong to queen mary about the year 1555 and might have be bring over from germany mary keep a pack of spaniel for hunting purpose there be another theory advance and with some reason that the english toy spaniel of the present day derive its origin from the cocker spaniel as these larger dog have the same color and marking black and tan tricolor and red and white the cocker also occasionally have the spot on the forehead which be a characteristic of the blenheim be the origin of the king charle spaniel and its advent in this country what it may king charle ii so much indulge and love these little friend that they follow he hither and thither as they pleased and seem to have be seldom separate from he by he they be love and cherish and bring into great popularity in he company they adorn canvas and ancient tapestry and be repute to have be allow free access at all time to whitehall hampton court and other royal palace there be now four recognize variety of the english toy spaniel or more properly speak five as the marlborough blenheim be consider a distinct type the latter be say by some to be the oldest of the toy spaniel by other to have be the evidence of vandyck watteau francoi boucher and greuze in whose picture they be so frequently introduce all the toy spaniel of bygone day have much longer nose and smaller flatter head than those of the present time and they have much longer ear these in many instance drag on the ground the marlborough blenheim have retain several of the ancestral point although this variety be of the same family and have the same name as the short-nosed blenheim of the present day there be a great deal of difference between the two type the marlborough be higher on the leg which need not be so fully feathered he have a much longer muzzle and a flatter and more contract skull the marlborough possess many of the attribute of a sport spaniel but so also do the modern blenheim although perhaps in a which h b chalon draw for daniel 's _ rural sport _ in 1801 this will bear out the statement that the smaller type of spaniel may be descend from the cocker the ground color of this dog be white with chestnut encircle the ear to the muzzle the side of the neck be chestnut as be also the ear there be a white blaze on the forehead in the center of which should be a clear lozenge-shaped chestnut spot call the beauty spot which by inbreed with other variety be fast be lose chestnut marking be on the body and on the side of the hind-leg the coat should incline to be curly the head must be flat not broad and the muzzle should be straight the chestnut should be of a rich color the four variety -- the king charle tricolor or -lrb- as he have be call -rrb- charle i spaniel the modern blenheim and the ruby -- have all the same point differ from one another in color only and the follow description of the point as determine by the toy spaniel club serve for all : -- \* \* \* \* \* head -- should be well domed and in good specimen be absolutely semi-globular sometimes even extend beyond the half-circle and project over the eye so as nearly to meet the upturned nose eye -- the eye be set wide apart with the eyelid square to the line of the face not oblique or fox-like the eye themselves be large and dark as possible so as to be generally consider black they enormous pupil which be absolutely of that color increase the description there be always a certain amount of weep show at the inner angle this be owe to a defect in the lachrymal duct stop -- the stop or hollow between the eye be well mark as in the bulldog or even more so some good specimen exhibit a hollow deep enough to bury a small marble nose -- the nose must be short and well turn up between the eye and without any indication of artificial displacement afford by a deviation to either side the color of the end should be black and it should be both deep and wide with open nostril jaw -- the muzzle must be square and deep and the lower jaw wide between the branch leave plenty of space for the tongue and for the attachment of the lower lip which should completely conceal the tooth it should also be turn up or finish so as to allow of its meet the end of the upper jaw turn up in a similar way as above describe ear -- the ear must be long so as to approach the ground in a average-sized dog they measure twenty inch from tip to tip and some reach twenty-two inch or even a trifle more they should be set low on the head hang flat to the side of the cheek and be heavily feathered in this last respect the king charle be expect to exceed the blenheim and he ear occasionally extend to twenty-four inch size -- the most desirable size be indicate by the accept weight of from 7 lb to 10 lb shape -- in compactness of shape these spaniel almost rival the pug but the length of coat add greatly to the apparent bulk as the body when the coat be wet look small in comparison with that dog still it ought to be decidedly cobby with strong stout leg short broad back and wide chest the symmetry of the king charle be of importance but it be seldom that there be any defect in this respect coat -- the coat find a absence of the long tail if leave intact the tail would take two or three year to attain perfection but the same may be say of the dog generally which improve very much with age and be not at its best until it be three year old and even then continue to improve although the toy spaniel be unquestionably true aristocrat by nature birth and breeding and be most at home in a drawing-room or of dog fear lest they might not find in another breed such engaging little friend and companion gentle as of yore and also comforter although these dog need care they possess great power of endurance they appreciate warmth and comfort but do not thrive so well in either extreme heat or intense cold one thing to be avoid be the wetting of they feathered foot or should this happen allow they to remain so and as in the case of all dog with long ear the interior of the ear should be carefully keep dry to avoid the risk of canker in go back to a period long before the last century be half-way adamson miss spofforth mr hope paterson mr lydium jenkin and miss e taylor the novice fancier desirous of breed for profit exhibition or pleasure when price be a object for consideration be often better advise to purchase a healthy puppy from a breeder of repute rather than to be delude with the notion that a good adult can be purchase for a few pound or to be carry away with the idea that a cheap indifferently breed specimen will produce first-class stock it take year to breed out bad point but good blood will tell when you be purchase a bitch with the intention of breed many inquiry should be make as to the stock from which she come this will influence the selection of the sire to whom she be to be mate and he should excel in the point in which she be deficient it be absolutely necessary to have perfectly healthy animal and if the female be young and small stock be desire she mate should be several year she senior a plain specimen of the right blood be quite likely to produce good result to the breeder for example should there be two female puppy in a well-bred litter one remarkable as promise to have all the requirement for a come champion the other large and plain this latter should be select for breed purpose as be stronger she will make a better and more useful mother than she handsome sister who should be keep for exhibition or for sale at a remunerative price the modern craze for small specimen make they quite unsuitable for procreation a brood bitch should not be less than 9 lb in weight and even heavier be preferable a sire the same size will produce small and far more typical stock than one of 5 lb or 6 lb as the tendency be to degenerate especially in head point but small size can be obtain by suitably select the parent the early spring be the best season for breed as it give the puppy a start of at least six month in which to grow and get strong before the cold weather set in although of course they can be breed at any time but autumn and winter puppy be more troublesome to rear it be always wise to administer occasionally both to puppy and adult a dose of worm medicine so as to give no chance to internal parasite -- the most troublesome ill with which the dog owner have to wrestle cause even more mortality than the dreaded scourge of distemper the rule of hygiene can not be overlook as upon they hang the success of the breeder plenty of fresh air light and sunshine be as necessary as food puppy of this breed be essentially delicate and must be keep free from cold and draught but they require liberty and freedom to develop and strengthen they limb otherwise they be liable to develop rickets they food should be of the best quality and after the age of six month nothing seem more suitable than stale brown breed cut up dice size and moisten with good stock gravy together with minced lean underdone roast beef with the addition two or three time a week of a little well-cooked green vegetable vary with rice or suet pudding and plain biscuit fish may also be give occasionally when only two or three dog be keep table scrap will generally be sufficient but the pernicious habit of feed at all time and give sweet pastry and rich dainty be most harmful and must produce disastrous result to the unfortunate animal two meal a day at regular interval be quite sufficient to keep these little pet in the best condition although puppy should be feed four time daily in small quantity after leave the mother they will thrive better if put on dry food and a small portion of scraped or finely mince lean meat give they every other day alternately with a chop hard-boiled egg and stale bread-crumb chapter xlv the pekinese and the japanese few of the many breed of foreign dog now establish in england common ailment of the dog and they treatment the experienced dog-owner have long ago realize that cleanliness wholesome food judicious exercise and a dry comfortable and well-ventilated kennel be the surest safeguard of health and that attention to these necessaries save he a infinitude of trouble and anxiety by protect he dog from disease on the first appearance of illness in he kennel the wise dog-owner at once call in the skill of a good veterinary surgeon but there be some of the minor ailment which he can deal with himself whilst he ought at least to be able to recognize the first symptom of the dreaded distemper and give first aid until the vet arrive to apply he remedy and give professional advice distemper although more than one hundred year have elapse since this be first import to this country from france a great amount of misunderstanding still prevail among a large section of dog-breeder regard its true nature and origin the fact be the disease come to we with a bad name for the french themselves deem it incurable in this country the old-fashioned plan of treatment be wont to be the usual rough remedy -- emetic purgative the seton and the lancet fail in this specifics of all sort be eagerly seek for and try and be unfortunately still believe in to a very great extent distemper have a certain course to run and in this disease nature seem to attempt the elimination of the poison through the secretion throw out by the naso-pharyngeal mucous membrane we chief difficulty in the treatment of distemper lie in the complication thereof we may and often do have the organ of respiration attack we have sometimes congestion of the liver or mucous inflammation of the bile duct or some lesion of the brain or nervous structure combine with epilepsy convulsion or chorea distemper be also often complicate with severe disease of the bowels and at time with a affection of the eye _ cause _ -- whether it be that the distemper virus the poison seedling of the disease really originate in the kennel or be the result of contact of one dog with another or whether the poison float to the kennel on the wing of the wind or be carry there on a shoe or the point of a walking-stick the follow fact ought to be bear in mind : -lrb- 1 -rrb- anything that debilitate the body or weaken the nervous system pave the way for the distemper poison -lrb- 2 -rrb- the healthier the dog the more power do he possess to resist contagion -lrb- 3 -rrb- when the disease be epizootic it can often be keep at bay by proper attention to diet and exercise frequent change of kennel straw and perfect cleanliness -lrb- 4 -rrb- the predispose cause which have come more immediately under my notice be debility cold damp starvation filthy kennel unwholesome food impure air and grief _ the age at which dog take distemper _ -- they may take distemper at any age the most common time of life be from the fifth till the eleventh or twelfth month _ symptom _ -- there be first and foremost a period of latency or of incubation in which there be more or less of dullness and loss of appetite and this glide gradually into a state of feverishness the fever may be usher in with chill and shiver the nose now become hot and dry the dog be restless and thirsty and the conjunctiva of the eye will be find to be considerably inject sometimes the bowels be at first constipated but they be more usually irregular sneezing will also be frequent and in some case cough dry and husky at first the temperature should be take and if there be a rise of two or three degree the case should be treat as distemper and not as a common cold at the commencement there be but little exudation from the eye and nose but as the disease advance this symptom will become more marked be clear at first so too will another symptom which be partially diagnostic of the malady namely increase heat of body combine with a rapid fall off in flesh sometimes indeed proceed quickly on to positive emaciation as the disease creep downward and inwards along the air-passage the chest get more and more affect the discharge of mucus and pus from the nostril more abundant and the cough lose its dry character become moist the discharge from the eye be simply mucus and pus but if not constantly dry away will gum the inflamed lid together that from the nostril be not only purulent but often mixed with dark blood the appetite be now clean go and there be often vomit and occasional attack of diarrhoea now in mild case we may look for some abatement of the symptom about the fourteenth day the fever get less inflammation decrease in the mucous passage and appetite be restore as one of the first sign of return health more often however the disease become complicated _ diagnosis _ -- the diagnostic symptom be the severe catarrh combine not only with fever but speedy emaciation _ pneumonia _ as we might easily imagine be a very likely complication and a very dangerous one there be great distress in breathe the animal panting rapidly the countenance be anxious the pulse small and frequent and the extremity cold the animal would fain sit up on he haunch or even seek to get out into the fresh air but sickness weakness and prostration often forbid he movement if the ear or stethoscope be apply to the chest the characteristic sign of pneumonia will be hear these be sound of moist crepitation etc _ bronchitis _ be probably the most common complication in fact it be always present except in very mild case the cough become more severe and often come on in tear paroxysm cause sickness and vomit the breathing be short and frequent the mouth hot and fill with viscid saliva while very often the bowels be constipate if the liver become involve we shall very soon have the jaundiced eye and the yellow skin _ diarrhoea _ be another very common complication we have frequent purge and maybe sickness and vomit _ fit _ of a convulsive character be frequent concomitant of distemper _ epilepsy _ be sometimes see owe no doubt to degeneration of the nerve centre cause by blood-poison there be many other complication and skin complaint be common after it _ treatment _ -- this consist firstly in do all in we power to guide the specific catarrhal fever to a safe termination and secondly in watch for and combate complication whenever we see a young dog ail lose appetite exhibit catarrhal symptom and get thin with a rise in temperature we should not lose a hour if he be a indoor dog find he a good bed in a clean well-ventilated apartment free from lumber and free from dirt if it be summer have all the window out or open if winter a little fire will be necessary but have half the window open at the same time only take precaution against he lie in a draught fresh air in case of distemper and indeed in fever of all kind can not be too highly extol the more rest the dog have the better he must be keep free from excitement and care must be take to guard he against cold and wet when he go out of door to obey the call of nature the most perfect cleanliness must be enjoin and disinfectant use such as permanganate of potash carbolic acid pearson 's or izal if the sick dog on the other hand be one of a kennel of dog then quarantine must be adopt the hospital should be quite remove from the vicinity of all other dog and as soon as the animal be take from the kennel the latter should be thoroughly cleanse and disinfect and the other dog keep warm and dry well feed and moderately exercise _ food and drink _ -- for the first three or four day let the food be light and easily digest in order to induce the animal to take it it should be as palatable as possible for small dog you can not have anything better than milk porridge -lrb- 1 -rrb- at all event the dog must if possible be induce to eat he must not be horn unless there be great emaciation he must not over-eat but what he get must be good as to drink dog usually prefer clean cold water and we can not do harm by mix therewith a little plain nitre -lrb- 1 -rrb- oatmeal porridge make with milk instead of water _ medicine _ -- begin by give a simple dose of castor oil just enough and no more than will clear out the bowels by one or two motion drastic purgative and medicine such as mercury jalap aloe and podophyllyn can not be too highly condemn for very small toy dog such as italian greyhound yorkshire terrier etc i should not recommend even oil itself but _ manna _ -- one drachm to two drachm dissolve in milk by simply get the bowels to act once or twice we shall have do enough for the first day and have only to make the dog comfortable for the night on the next day begin with a mixture such as the following : solution of acetate of ammonia 30 drop to 120 sweet spirit of nitre 15 drop to 60 salicylate of soda 2 grain to 10 thrice daily in a little camphor water if the cough be very troublesome and the fever do not run very high the follow may be substitute for this on the second or third day : syrup of squill 10 drop to 60 tincture of henbane 10 drop to 60 sweet spirit of nitre 10 drop to 60 in camphor water a few drop of dilute hydrochloric acid should be add to the and warm water twelve hour afterwards chapter lii the dog and the law privilege of first bite it be popularly but rather erroneously suppose that _ every _ dog be entitle to one bite perhaps it would be more accurate to state that every dog may with impunity have one snap or one intended bite but only dog of hitherto irreproachable character be permit the honor of a genuine tasteful bite once a dog however have display dangerous propensity even though he have never have the satisfaction of effect a actual bite and once he owner or the person who harbor he become aware of these evil inclination -lrb- scienter -rrb- either of he own knowledge or by notice the law look upon such dog as a dangerous beast which the owner keep at he peril the onus of proof be on the victim to show that the owner have previous knowledge of the animal 's ferocity though in reality very little evidence of scienter be as a rule require and notice need not necessarily be give directly to the owner but to any person who have charge of the dog the person attack have yet another remedy he can if he be able kill the dog before it can bite he but he be not justified in shoot the animal as it run away even _ after _ be bite by 28 and 29 vict c 60 the owner of a dog which attack sheep or cattle -- and cattle include horse -- be responsible for all damage and there be no necessity to prove previous evil propensity this act be wholly repeal by the act call the dog ' act 1906 which come into force on January 1st 1907 but the new act re-enact the section have reference to damage to cattle and say that in such case it be not necessary for the person claim damages to show a previous mischievous propensity in the dog or the owner 's knowledge of such previous propensity or to show that the injury be attributable to neglect on the part of the owner the word cattle include horse ass sheep goat and swine the law look upon fight between dog as a natural and necessary incident in the career of every member of the canine race and give no redress to the owner of the vanquish animal provide the fight be a fair one and the contestant appear to consider it so the owner however of a peaceably disposed dog which be attack and injure or kill by one savage and unrestrained have a right of action against the owner of the latter the owner of the peaceably disposed animal may justifiably kill the savage brute in order to save he dog but he must run the risk of be able to prove that this be the only means of put a stop to the fight licence every dog owner must annually take out a licence for each dog he keep the licence which be obtainable at all post-office at the cost of 7 6d be date to run from the hour it be take out until the follow 31st December the person in whose custody or upon whose premise the dog be find will be deem its owner until prove otherwise the owner of certain dog for certain purpose be however exempt from take out licence viz : -lrb- 1 -rrb- dog under the age of six month -lrb- 2 -rrb- hound under twelve month old neither use nor hunt with the pack provide that the master have take out proper licence for all hound enter in the pack -lrb- 3 -rrb- one dog keep and use by a blind person solely for he or she guidance -lrb- 4 -rrb- dog keep and use solely for the purpose of tend sheep or cattle or in the exercise of the occupation or calling from stray during all or any of the hour between sunset and sunrise stray dog the dog act 1906 have some important section deal with seizure of stray dog and enact that where a police officer have reason to believe that any dog find in a highway or place of public resort be a stray dog he may seize and retain it until the owner have claim it and pay all expense incur by reason of its detention if the dog so seize wear a collar on which be the address of any person or if the owner of the dog be know then the chief officer of police or some person authorise by he in that behalf shall serve on either such person a notice in write state that the dog have be seize and will be sell or destroy if not claim within seven clear day of the service of the notice fail the owner put in a appearance and pay all expense of detention within the seven clear day then the chief officer of police or any person authorise by he may cause the dog to be sell or destroy in a manner to cause as little pain as possible the police must keep a proper register of all dog seize and every such register shall be open to inspection at all reasonable time by any member of the public on payment of a fee of one shilling and the police may transfer such dog to any establishment for the reception of stray dog but only if there be a proper register keep at such establishment open to inspection by the public on payment of a fee not exceed one shill another section enact that any person who take possession of a stray dog shall forthwith either return the dog to its owner or give notice in write to the chief officer of police of the district where the dog be find contain a description of the dog and state the place where the dog be find and the place where he be be detain and any person fail to comply with the provision of this section shall be liable on conviction under the summary jurisdiction act to a fine not exceed forty shilling importation of dog the power of make order deal with the importation of dog be vest in the board of agriculture who have absolute authority in the matter the initial step to be take by a person wish to import any dog into great britain from any other country except ireland the channel island and the isle of man be that he must fill up a application form to the say board which he have previously obtain from they in which he apply for a licence to land the dog under the condition impose by the board which he undertake to obey on the form he have to give a full description of the dog the name and address of the owner the propose port of landing and the approximate date of landing and further from list which he will receive from the board he must select the carry agent he propose should superintend the movement of the dog from the port of landing to the place of detention and also the premise of a veterinary surgeon on which he propose the dog shall be detain and isolate as require by the order a import dog must be land and take to its place of detention in a suitable box hamper crate or other receptacle and as a general rule have to remain entirely isolate for a period of six month motor car and dog unquestionably the greatest enemy that the dog possess at the present time be the motor car presume the owner of the dog be fortunate enough to know whose car it be that run over he dog and to have some evidence of excessive or unreasonable speed or other negligence on the part of the car driver at the time of the accident he will find the law ever ready to assist he a dog have every bit as much right to the high road as a motor car effort have be make on the part of motor owner to get the court to hold that dog on a high road be only under proper control if on a lead and that if they be not on a lead the owner of they be guilty of negligence in allow he dog to stroll about and therefore be not entitle to recover : such effort have not be successful even suppose a court to hold that the fact of a dog be loose in this way or unaccompanied be evidence of negligence against he owner this would by no means defeat he owner 's claim for the law be that though a plaintiff may have be negligent in some such way as
